        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/aenm.202303786')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'acc02528-6cfa-4c0f-aeca-26595ceb34ff'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Xu/Jie">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Cheng/Yuwen" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yuwen Cheng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Cheng/Yuwen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Liu/Haimei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a10" data-db-target-for="a10" aria-controls="a10" aria-haspopup="true" id="a10_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Haimei Liu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a10" aria-labelledby="a10_Ctrl" role="region" id="a10">

                     <p>Shanghai Key Laboratory of Materials Protection and Advanced Materials in Electric Power, College of Environmental and Chemical Engineering, Shanghai University of Electric Power, Shanghai, 200090 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Liu/Haimei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Guo/Ziyang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a11" data-db-target-for="a11" aria-controls="a11" aria-haspopup="true" id="a11_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Ziyang Guo<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a11" aria-labelledby="a11_Ctrl" role="region" id="a11">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d103785881" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), based on hard carbon anodes and Na<sup>+</sup>-intercalation compound cathodes, have gained significant attention. Nonetheless, hard carbon anodes involve the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> at a low potential, typically below 0.1&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), which increases the risk of dendritic Na growth on the anode surface during overcharging. Herein, a safe organic/inorganic composite anode containing tetrasodium 3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylate (Na<sub>4</sub>PTC) and Metallic bismuth (Bi) with a weight ratio of 7:2, which exhibits an average potential of 0.7&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a capacity of 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is proposed. The electrode reaction involves a reversible coordination reaction within the organic host and alloying reactions within the metallic Bi component. Importantly, the organic component efficiently buffers the volume changes in Bi during the alloying reaction, while the metallic Bi enhances the electronic conductivity of the organic material. As a result, this composite anode shows high cycle stability and rate performance, even under high mass loadings ranging from 10 to 50&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Furthermore, it is demonstrated that the Na-ion full cell, consisting of the composite anode and the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F cathode, exhibits minimal capacity degradation over 100 cycles while maintaining a high areal capacity of 1.1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303786-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303786-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) find widespread use in portable electronic devices, electric vehicles (EVs), and smart grids, raising concerns about the limited availability of lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0001_R_d103785873e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater. Joule Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Adv. Mater. Joule">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), known for their abundant resources, have become a hot research topic in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0002_R_d103785873e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Science Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. J. Electrochem.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to LIBs, the cathode materials of SIBs are mainly insertion compounds, including layered oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0003_R_d103785873e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> poly-anionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0004_R_d103785873e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue analogs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0005_R_d103785873e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the commonly used graphite anode in LIBs cannot be used to store Na<sup>+</sup>. Currently, the optimal choice for the anode of SIBs is still hard carbon. The discharge curve of hard carbon can be divided into two regions: a plateau region (below 0.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a sloping region (above 0.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), with the capacity in the plateau region often exceeding 60% of the total capacity of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0006_R_d103785873e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Small Small Adv. Mater. ACS Nano">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During fast charging of SIBs, polarization effects can cause the voltage of the hard carbon anode plateau region to shift downward, leading to the precipitation of metallic sodium and the growth of sodium dendrites, resulting in safety issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0007_R_d103785873e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is necessary to explore the anode materials with higher Na<sup>+</sup> insertion potentials to enable the development of safer and higher-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, researchers have developed numerous anode materials with higher Na<sup>+</sup> insertion potentials, such as conversion/alloying materials (TiO<sub>2</sub>, SnO<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>, Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, etc.) and alloy materials (Sb, Red P, Black P, Bi, etc.).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0008_R_d103785873e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Nano Energy Environ. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc. ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these anode materials, while possessing higher Na<sup>+</sup> insertion potentials, also face their own shortcomings, including cycling instability, poor reversibility, low resource abundance, or excessively high voltage. Apart from the aforementioned anode materials, in recent years, organic electrode materials have garnered widespread attention from researchers due to their advantages of sustainability, structural diversity, and design flexibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0009_R_d103785873e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Joule Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these organic materials have primarily been used as cathode materials, with only a limited number of reported applications in anodes. Recently, Song and co-workers<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0010_R_d103785873e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported an organic anode material, tetrasodium 3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylate (Na<sub>4</sub>PTC), which exhibits suitable charge/discharge potentials (0.78/0.74&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), making it a safer anode material for SIBs. However, organic materials often exhibit poor electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0011_R_d103785873e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Energy ACS Energy Lett. Nat. Commun. ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC anode relies on a high carbon content (20&nbsp;wt.%) and low mass loading (1–2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), which significantly deviates from the practical electrode configurations (typically ≈10&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). It is well-known that low mass loading of active materials increases the proportion of non-active materials in the battery, such as current collectors, separators, and the battery casing. Additionally, the use of a large amount of high-surface-area conductive carbon leads to high electrode porosity, requiring a substantial amount of electrolyte to maintain high ion conductivity, further reducing the energy density of the battery. Furthermore, the extensive use of high-surface-area conductive carbon significantly reduces the volumetric capacity of the electrode, thereby lowering the volumetric energy density of the battery.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we propose a highly safe organic/inorganic composite anode for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, consisting of a 7:2 weight ratio blend of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC and Bi. This composite exhibits an average Na<sup>+</sup>-storage potential of 0.6&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a capacity of 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism involving the reversible transformation between R─C═O and R─C─Na<sup>+</sup> within the organic host (Na<sub>4</sub>PTC), in conjunction with the alloying reaction occurring within the metallic host (Bi). We demonstrate that the organic component efficiently mitigates the volume changes induced by the alloying reaction within the Bi component, while the metallic Bi simultaneously counters the low electronic conductivity inherent in the organic component. Benefitting from the synergistic effects of organic and inorganic components, this composite anode demonstrates exceptional cycle stability and rate performance, even at a high mass loading range of 10 to 50&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, far surpassing the performance of the majority of previously reported organic electrodes. Then, we assemble a 3&nbsp;V Na-ion full battery by combining the organic/inorganic composite anode with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F (NVOPF) cathode. This battery exhibits negligible capacity degradation over 100 cycles while maintaining a high areal capacity of 1.1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> within the electrodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303786-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303786-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The hydrolysis reaction of 3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) in NaOH solution successfully yielded Na<sub>4</sub>PTC. Verifications of the synthesized product's structure and purity are performed by various characterization techniques, such as X-ray diffraction (XRD), 1H Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy and Thermogravimetry Analysis (TG) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the&nbsp;Scanning electron microscope (SEM) image shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) indicates that the synthesized Na<sub>4</sub>PTC exhibits a short rod-shaped morphology with a length of ≈2&nbsp;µm. In previous explorations, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC has been investigated by theoretical calculations,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0008_R_d103785873e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Edit. Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Nano Energy Environ. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc. ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the relevant experimental solid evidence is still lacking. Therefore, in situ Fourier transform infrared attenuated total reflection (FTIR/ATR) spectroscopy is first used to investigate the reaction mechanism of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC in sodium batteries. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve within the window of 0.1–1.5&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 0.3&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> is used to characterize the electrochemical behavior of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC and its functional groups variations have also been in situ recorded by FTIR/ATR test at the same time over-discharge/charge process (the left of <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0001">1a</a>). The corresponding 2D color-filled contour plot of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC-related FTIR peaks at different potentials is presented on the right of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0001">1a</a>, and the corresponding 3D diagram of the above in situ FTIR/ATR spectra is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) (PS: the FTIR spectra collected at the open-circuit state is used as the background to evaluate the changes of different functional groups in the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC anode during the redox process). Notably, the intensity of the characteristic peaks at 1285, 1350, and 1548&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> weakens significantly during the reduction process, indicating the decrease of C─C. Furthermore, the signal at 1423 and 1582&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> also weakens corresponding to the decrease of C═O. Conversely, the rise of signal intensity at 1392 and 1600–1650&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> represents a substantial enhancement of C═C during the reduction process. The signal at 1489&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> assigned to the C═C bond in the benzene ring may arise. This is because during the reduction process, although the total number of C═C in the molecule increases, the number of C═C on the benzene ring decreases. Hence, we conclude that Na<sub>4</sub>PTC releases or stores Na<sup>+</sup> through the reaction between C═O and C─ONa during the redox process. Intriguingly, a continuously enhanced signal appearing at 1210–1270&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> catches our attention, representing C─O─C in cyclic anhydride. Therefore, we speculate that the molecular changes during the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC reduction process are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0001">1b</a>: in addition to the conversion from C═O to C─ONa, there is also the generation of C─O─C, which implies the involvement of only two C═O in the reaction. Ex situ Raman spectroscopy and XRD patterns are also employed to investigate the changes of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC over charge/discharge process. Reversible changes of C═O→C─ONa (1300 and 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and C═C→C─C (1575&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) are also observed by ex situ Raman measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0001">1c</a>). The ex situ XRD results show that the interplanar spacings of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC gradually decrease during the discharge process, and fully recover during the charge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303786-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0eb46009-58ba-4037-98cc-2db8f0925289/aenm202303786-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0eb46009-58ba-4037-98cc-2db8f0925289/aenm202303786-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4dfc79ef-63be-4733-be5d-58bda992c850/aenm202303786-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0eb46009-58ba-4037-98cc-2db8f0925289/aenm202303786-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303786-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303786" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curve in current versus time form and corresponding 2D color-filled contour plot in wave number versus time form of the in situ FTIR/ATR test of the Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC. b) Schematic diagram for the proposed reversible electrochemical redox mechanism of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC. c) Ex situ Raman spectroscopy of Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>After gaining a clear understanding for the sodium storage mechanism of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, we proceeded to assess its electrochemical performance. Due to the poor conductivity of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, we introduced 20% Ketjen black (KB) as conductive agent and conducted electrochemical performance with low electrode loading (2–3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). The subsequent reported specific capacity of half cell, labeled as “Specific Capacity (mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>)<sub>total</sub>,” are calculated based on the total electrode loading, encompassing the active material, binder, and conductive agent. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2a</a>, the Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC (2–3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) system exhibits outstanding rate performance, retaining 63% of the initial discharge capacity (corresponding to the specific capacity of 77&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) even at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The results from cyclic voltammetry (CV) data at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> also demonstrate fast reaction kinetics of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC exhibits excellent cycling stability with a capacity retention rate of 91.8% after 1600 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2b</a>). The charge/discharge curves of the first two cycles for Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), indicating an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 64% and a charging plateau ≈0.8&nbsp;V. Based on the excellent cycling stability of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, we inferred that it is insoluble in the electrolyte. To verify this, we immersed the pristine Na<sub>4</sub>PTC electrode and the discharged Na<sub>4</sub>PTC electrode in the electrolyte for 24&nbsp;h and characterized them by using UV–vis spectra. Consistent with our expectations, the UV–vis signal of the electrolyte soaked with the pristine electrode is identical to that of the bare electrolyte. Surprisingly, there is a significant change in the UV–vis signal of the electrolyte that had been soaked with the discharged-state electrode. This suggests that Na<sub>4</sub>PTC is insoluble in the electrolyte, but its discharge products are soluble in the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). To further estimate the solubility of discharge products, the discharged electrodes with active material loading of 1.34, 2.68, and 4.02&nbsp;mg were immersed in 3&nbsp;ml electrolyte for 24&nbsp;h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information). The supernatants of these above-mixed solutions were collected for UV–vis test. Interestingly, the curves derived from the UV–vis spectra of three as-prepared supernatants are identical, indicating that the discharge products of 1.34&nbsp;mg can not be completely dissolved in 3&nbsp;mL of electrolyte. Therefore, we estimate that the solubility of discharge products in the electrolyte is less than 0.44&nbsp;mg&nbsp;mL<sup>−1</sup>. Considering that only 50&nbsp;µL electrolytes are added in actual battery testing, a maximum of 0.022&nbsp;mg discharge products can be dissolved (occupying 1% of the active material). Compared to the entire electrode, these losses are negligible. The effect of this slight dissolution can be observed by keeping the battery in the discharged state for 24&nbsp;h. After 24&nbsp;h, the capacity loss in the recharge process is only 8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information), and in the next cycle, some capacity is reactivated, resulting in an actual capacity loss of less than 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information). The above results indicate that the slight dissolution of discharge products has a minimal impact on the battery performance. Na<sub>4</sub>PTC demonstrates excellent electrochemical performance, and we also provide an explanation for its reasons. However, this superior electrochemical performance can only be achieved under low electrode loading conditions; once the electrode loading is increased, the electrochemical performance sharply deteriorates. Moreover, the application of a significant amount of conductive carbon results in a low energy density. The specific capacity decreases by 30% when calculated according to the whole electrode loading. This is a common issue for OEMs. What's more, the introduction of a large amount of conductive carbon can also hinder the achievement of high loading in OEMs or result in poor performance of OEMs under high-loading conditions. The presence of a large amount of conductive carbon leads to a high electrode porosity. In our experiments, regardless of the electrode loading, the uniform electrolyte addition is set at 50&nbsp;µL. Therefore, as the electrode loading increases, the electrolyte is insufficient to completely fill the electrode pores, resulting in incomplete redox reactions of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC. When the loading is further increased to 20&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, the rate performance of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC electrode drops significantly, and the Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC system can no longer operate at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2c</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2d</a>, when the cycling stability of a thick electrode is tested at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC initially exhibits almost no capacity, and the capacity slowly increases with the number of cycles, but the maximum capacity reaches only 31.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2d</a>). Additionally, frequent overcharging phenomena are observed after just 50 cycles. The corresponding CE dates are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The above results indicate that under conditions of high conductive carbon content, high-loading Na<sub>4</sub>PTC electrode can hardly operate. These results highlight the current challenges faced by OEMs. As such, further research is necessary to explore new approaches to overcome these problems.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303786-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/96e1ec22-f870-481c-91a5-e3f632f48f31/aenm202303786-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/96e1ec22-f870-481c-91a5-e3f632f48f31/aenm202303786-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e7839e2-98af-48e4-9b31-b908fa51d445/aenm202303786-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/96e1ec22-f870-481c-91a5-e3f632f48f31/aenm202303786-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303786-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303786" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rate performance and cycling stability of Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC with a, b) low and c, d) high loading.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Combining organic materials with conductive active materials may indeed be an effective approach to reduce electrode carbon content and also increase electrode capacity. Bi has a sodium storage potential (0.7/0.5&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) similar to that of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, while also possessing relatively high capacity (385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). We prepared the composite material NB by mixing Na<sub>4</sub>PTC and Bi in a mass ratio of 7:2. Subsequently, we prepared the NB electrodes using a mass ratio of 9:0.5:0.5 (90% NB, 5%&nbsp;KB, 5% CMC). For comparisons, we also constructed the contrast electrodes with different carbon contents (70N: 70% Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, 20%&nbsp;KB, 10% CMC; 90N: 90% Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, 5%&nbsp;KB, 5% CMC). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3a</a>, we investigate the effect of carbon content on the electrode porosity, which is found to reduce with decreasing carbon content and increasing Bi content. Furthermore, the accumulation of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC particles leading to a relatively high porosity is advantageous for mitigating the volume expansion of Bi during the alloying reaction process. We further examine the relationship between the amount of electrolyte and the mass ratio of active material for different electrode porosities, revealing that decreasing the carbon content can significantly reduce the amount of electrolyte and improve the energy density of the cell. The electrolyte volume is calculated based on the electrode porosity, determining the minimum electrolyte volume required to fill all the pores. To gain a clearer understanding of the advantages of composite materials in terms of electrical conductivity, we conducted the further tests on the electrical conductivity of the electrode materials with different ratios. During conductivity testing, the binder CMC is removed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3b</a>, the bare KB has high conductivity, and the conductivities of the electrode materials enhance with the increase of the conductive carbon content. Despite the suboptimal electronic conductivity of Bi, it is capable of demonstrating a stable capacity of 360&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (theoretical capacity: 385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) without adding any conductive agent (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, even though the conductivity of metallic Bi is not ideal, it is adequate to showcase its electrochemical performance. In addition, NB exhibits an order of magnitude improvement in electronic conductivity compared to 90&nbsp;N, indicating the high electronic conductivity of the organic/inorganic composite material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303786-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/75cd1fad-9bdc-44bd-baeb-ebed44dae9f6/aenm202303786-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/75cd1fad-9bdc-44bd-baeb-ebed44dae9f6/aenm202303786-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/496e21fd-62cd-4e9b-8d05-d0234648e3b3/aenm202303786-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/75cd1fad-9bdc-44bd-baeb-ebed44dae9f6/aenm202303786-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303786-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303786" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The relationship of porosity and E/AM (electrolyte/active materials). b) Investigation of electronic conductivity of Ketjen black (KB), Bi, 70N, NB and 90N powders using four-pin probe method. c) Evolution of density of states (DOS) of Bi, NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. Na and Bi projections are colored in orange and cyan, respectively. d) Time–voltage curves of in situ EIS. e–g) DRT analysis of the impedance date at the selected position in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0002">2d</a>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>We have demonstrated the advantages of the composite electrode materials in reducing electrode porosity and enhancing electrode conductivity. However, the organic/inorganic composite material still have two potential risks: reduction in electronic conductivity as the alloying reaction of Bi continues, and volume expansion of Bi during the alloying process which may result in poor contact between the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC and the conductive agent. To assess the conductivity of Bi at various states, we employ first-principles calculations to evaluate the state density of Bi under different discharge states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The high electronic conductivity is maintained throughout the discharge process (Bi→NaBi→Na<sub>3</sub>Bi), thus maintaining an efficient conductive network throughout the redox process of the composite electrode. SEM images of the cycled electrode reveal the formation of a porous network</p>
         
         <p>structure of Bi (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0012_R_d103785873e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the mapping images of the NB electrode before and after cycling indicate that the signal distribution of Bi is more concentrated around the obvious porous network structure after cycling. The signal of the pristine electrode is relatively uniform (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). This change may result in poor contact between the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, Bi and conductive carbon, leading to an increase in resistance after cycling. We investigate the effect of this change in detail by in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscop (EIS). The corresponding time–voltage curves during the in situ EIS are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3d</a>. In situ EIS is conducted on the cell after the second and the 12th cycles. All EIS data are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). The distribution of relaxation time (DRT) analysis is performed to capture subtle changes in the EIS data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3e–g</a>). The DRT curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3e–g</a> correspond one-to-one with the selected EIS date in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3d</a>. The discharge plateau observed at ≈0.76&nbsp;V in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3d</a> is assigned to the reduction reaction of the Na<sub>4</sub>PTC, while the plateau at 0.74&nbsp;V represents the conversion from Bi to NaBi and the plateau at 0.5&nbsp;V correspond to the conversion from NaBi to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. The DRT analysis is also employed to investigate the changes of EIS in the reduction reaction of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC before and after nine cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3e</a>, the resistance of the NB electrode after nine cycles is slightly higher than its initial value. Nonetheless, the increase in impedance is not significant, and the performance of the NB electrode may not be overly negatively impacted. The DRT curve of the composite electrode at the Bi to NaBi reaction stage exhibits a slight increase in resistance as the reaction proceeded (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3f</a>). On the other hand, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3g</a>, the resistance slight decreases during the NaBi to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi reaction, while the diffusion rate increases significantly as the reaction progresses, suggesting the complete formation of the porous structure. This phenomenon is not found in the conversion reaction of Bi→NaBi, suggesting that the formation of porous structures may occur mainly in the conversion reaction of NaBi→Na<sub>3</sub>Bi rather than in the whole reaction process. The above results clearly indicate that the potential risks associated with the composite material are negligible.</p>
         
         <p>Subsequently, the Na//NB half cell is assembled to investigate the electrochemical performance of the composite material. The excellent rate performance of the Na//NB battery is demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a> (Supporting Information) (2–3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), where it retains 62% of its capacity (corresponding to the specific capacity of 128&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a corresponding charge/discharge time of only 27&nbsp;s. The introduction of Bi maintains the exceptional rate performance of the NB electrode. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a> (Supporting Information), the Na//NB cell exhibits superior cycling stability at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and furthermore, the capacity is increased by 70% compared to the Na//Na<sub>4</sub>PTC (The capacity is calculated based on the entire electrode loading mass). Meanwhile, the stable cycling performance of the Na//NB suggests that although the volume change of Bi during the charge/discharge processes may have some effect on the electrode structure, its negative impact on the electrode performance is negligible. This may be attributed to the appropriate electrode porosity, which provides a buffer for the volume expansion of Bi, consistent with the prediction presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0003">3</a>. The charge/discharge curves of the first two cycles for the Na//NB cell are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a> (Supporting Information), with an ICE of 70%. The oxidation/reduction potentials of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC are completely separated from those of Bi, making it easy to distinguish their respective capacity contributions. Although Bi accounts for only 20% of the content in the NB electrode, it contributes 55% of the capacity due to its high theoretical capacity (385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This clearly demonstrates the role of the conductive active</p>
         
         <p>materials in the composite electrodes. Due to the utilization of excess conductive carbon, the organic electrodes often cannot maintain comparable performance under high-loading conditions. The superiority of the composite electrodes is further verified by testing under high-loading. In the process of preparing high-loading electrodes, it is necessary to conduct roll pressing on the electrode to reduce the internal resistance. However, CMC has significant brittleness, and after roll pressing, it inevitably leads to the collapse of the electrode structure, resulting in issues such as powder shedding and exposed copper foil. Since the CMC binder fails to meet the requirements of high-loading electrodes, we substitute CMC with an equal amount of PTFE binder and press the active material onto a copper mesh for the similar electrochemical measurement. The corresponding electrochemical data of the NB/PTFE electrodes with low mass loading (2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), which show the slight increasing capacity and better cycling stability compared with the NB/CMC electrode. After substituting the binder with PTFE, the active material is effectively integrated into the copper mesh, facilitating enhanced electrode compaction. Consequently, this modification leads to a notable reduction in internal resistance within the battery and subsequently yields a higher capacity. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4a</a>, the Na//NB demonstrates excellent rate performance even with high electrode loading (10&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). The Na//NB retains 50% of its capacity (corresponding to the specific capacity of 152&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (6.6C). Even at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, Na//NB maintains low polarization. Na//NB also shows excellent cycle stability, keeping 90.5% of the initial capacity even after 1900 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4b</a>). At the same time, in 1900 cycles, Na//NB consistently maintains a Coulombic efficiency (CE) close to 100%. Even under a high loading of 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, the NB electrode can still maintain 34% of its capacity (corresponding to the specific capacity of 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4c</a>). Na//NB also retains 89.8% of the initial capacity after 1300 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating excellent cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4d</a>). With the electrode mass loading further increasing to 30&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (4.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), the NB electrode can still stably work for 500 cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4e</a>). Surprisingly, even at a loading of 50&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (6.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), the NB electrode still maintains 75% of the capacity for the light electrode with a loading of 2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and stably cycles 200 times (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0004">4f</a>). The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves for the NB electrodes loaded with different amounts of active materials at various cycle numbers are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). The remarkable effects achieved in reducing carbon content and increasing electrode loading through the use of conductive active materials in the preparation of organic/inorganic composite materials represent a groundbreaking advancement in the literature concerning organic anode materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303786-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303786-bib-0013_R_d103785873e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Small Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ACS Nano">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303786-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2540678-0589-44b1-8453-dcaef3100ecd/aenm202303786-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2540678-0589-44b1-8453-dcaef3100ecd/aenm202303786-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e7df4a90-3aba-4f16-a4e1-d9b5b12f6b11/aenm202303786-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2540678-0589-44b1-8453-dcaef3100ecd/aenm202303786-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303786-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303786" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a, c) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at different current densities and b, d) cycling stability with different high loading. Cycling stability e) 30 and f) 50&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) of Na//NB.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further demonstrate the advantages of the NB electrode, we selected the NVOPF cathode to match with the NB anode and assembled a high-loading full cell. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0005">5a</a>, the high-loading full cell demonstrates excellent rate performance, maintaining 50% of its capacity (corresponding to the specific capacity of 1.15&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a charging time of only 5&nbsp;min. The corresponding charge/discharge curves are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0005">5b</a>, revealing that even with increasing current, the full cell maintains an average discharge voltage of 2.75&nbsp;V at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Next, we assessed the cycling stability of the full cell at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After the initial capacity decay in the first few cycles, the full cell maintains a capacity of 1.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, exhibiting stable cycling for ≈100 cycles with minimal capacity loss (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0005">5c</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303786-fig-0005">5d</a>, the full cell exhibits an average discharge voltage of 3&nbsp;V, and throughout the cycling process, the charge/discharge curves almost coincide exactly. The above results demonstrate the potential of the NB electrode in various electrochemical energy storage devices.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303786-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/348563d8-32cc-48a5-8fd3-41d4f0b69284/aenm202303786-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/348563d8-32cc-48a5-8fd3-41d4f0b69284/aenm202303786-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/11cfbb8c-213f-498f-bbb9-55cba4f232f6/aenm202303786-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/348563d8-32cc-48a5-8fd3-41d4f0b69284/aenm202303786-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303786-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303786" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate performance and b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the NB//NVOPF at different current densities. c) Cycling stability and d) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the NB//NVOPF with high loading at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303786-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303786-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have successfully prepared a novel organic/inorganic (Na<sub>4</sub>PTC/Bi) composite material, which exhibit excellent electrochemical performance even under harsh testing conditions. The introduction of metallic Bi effectively reduces the demand of conductive carbon from 20 to 5&nbsp;wt.% for the NB electrode, while also lowers the porosity of the composite electrodes and increases their capacity. In addition, the appropriate porosity derived from the agglomeration of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC nanoparticles assists in mitigating the volume expansion during the Bi alloying process. The reaction mechanism of Na<sub>4</sub>PTC is investigated by in situ FTIR/ATR, ex situ Raman, and ex situ XRD. The in situ EIS combined with DRT analysis and the results of UV–vis spectroscopy demonstrated that the reason for the stable cycling of the composite material. Additionally, the composite material can achieve an electrode loading of 50&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, indicating that this strategy provides guidance for the practical development of the organic anode materials. NVOPF is selected as the cathode material and assembled with the NB electrode to construct a high-loading full cell. The full battery exhibits a surface capacity of 1.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and operates at a work voltage of 3&nbsp;V. Remarkably, the cell demonstrates stable cycling behavior, maintaining its performance over 100 cycles. This strategy of using conductive active materials to replace conductive carbon provides a reference for further exploration of low-cost and more practical organic batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
